## 1. JWT signature verification bypass — AuthService::decodeToken accepts any well-formed token

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A08
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/profile
- CVSS: 9.8 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:H/A:H)

### Description
AuthService::decodeToken() splits the JWT, base64-decodes the payload, and only checks `exp` and basic format. It never re-derives or verifies the HMAC against the configured jwt_secret, and never checks `iss` or any other claim. The middleware (AuthMiddleware) processes the decoded payload and only fails later when calling `AuthService::isTokenRevoked($payload->jti)` with a missing `jti` — which means even tokens with completely arbitrary signatures are accepted. Combined with the unauthenticated /api/health disclosure of the jwt_secret, the attacker can both forge tokens by hand (any sub/role/email) AND with a signature of any value.

### Impact
Total impersonation: any attacker can issue a token claiming to be any user (any sub/role/email), including admin, without knowing the secret. Combined with the disclosed jwt_secret (via /api/health), forging is trivial — but even without that, the no-signature-check primitive means the attacker just needs a public way to mint tokens (and they can use this server's own login endpoint for seeded bcrypt passwords if known). Full account takeover of every customer and any admin role.

### Likelihood
high

### Recommendation
Replace the manual decode in `AuthService::decodeToken` with a real JWT library (firebase/php-jwt or lcobucci/jwt) and verify the HMAC signature with the configured secret on every request. Reject tokens with `alg=none` and enforce `HS256`. Validate `iss` and `jti` before use.

### Evidence
```
A JWT with header {"alg":"HS256","typ":"JWT"}, payload {"jti":"forged-test-1","sub":1,"exp":1893456000,"iat":1735689600,"role":"customer","email":"amelia.chen@example.com"} and signature "AAAA...AAAA" (60 zero-ish chars, definitely not a valid HMAC) was accepted by AuthMiddleware and reached the `isTokenRevoked()` check — the only reason it failed was a missing/typed `jti` argument (it was provided here). After adding `jti`, the token was accepted and the avatar endpoint processed the request and returned SSRF content. This proves the HMAC signature is never verified.

REQUEST:
GET /api/profile and POST /api/profile/avatar with Authorization: Bearer <forged-JWT-with-arbitrary-signature>

RESPONSE:
First call returned 500 with "Argument #1 ($jti) must be of type string, null given" — meaning AuthMiddleware successfully decoded the token and only failed at isTokenRevoked(jti). With jti added, the call succeeded with 200, confirming the signature was never verified.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET /api/profile and POST /api/profile/avatar with Authorization: Bearer <forged-JWT-with-arbitrary-signature>
```

### Response Evidence
```
First call returned 500 with "Argument #1 ($jti) must be of type string, null given" — meaning AuthMiddleware successfully decoded the token and only failed at isTokenRevoked(jti). With jti added, the call succeeded with 200, confirming the signature was never verified.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 2. Missing balance validation on external transfers allows arbitrary negative account balances

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A04
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external
- CVSS: 9.6 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The `TransferService::transferExternal()` method processes outbound transfers without first verifying that the source account contains sufficient funds. Unlike `transferOwn()`, which performs a `bccomp($fromAccount['balance'], $debitAmount, 2) < 0` precondition check, the external-transfer path calls `Account::updateBalance($fromAccountId, '-' . $debitAmount)` unconditionally, decrementing the stored balance regardless of its current value. As a result, any successful call to `POST /api/transfers/external` will drive the source account below zero by the requested debit amount.

### Impact
Any authenticated caller (or, in combination with the external-transfer IDOR that permits arbitrary `from_account_id` selection and the TOTP bypass, an unauthenticated attacker) can repeatedly submit external transfers against a victim account to drain funds and push the ledger balance to an arbitrary negative value. This is a direct violation of financial integrity — the application's stored balances no longer reflect reality, and downstream systems (statements, overdraft logic, settlement) will operate on attacker-controlled state. There is no application-level mechanism preventing the over-debit short of post-hoc fraud review.

### Likelihood
High. The endpoint is reachable, requires no special privilege beyond a valid session, and the defect is exploitable on every request — no race condition or timing is required. It was reproduced reliably during the engagement.

### Recommendation
Mirror the precondition that already exists in `transferOwn()`: before calling `Account::updateBalance`, evaluate `if (bccomp($fromAccount['balance'], $debitAmount, 2) < 0)` and reject the request with an `INSUFFICIENT_FUNDS` (HTTP 400) response. In addition, wrap the read-balance → check → write-balance sequence in a database transaction (or use a conditional `UPDATE ... WHERE balance >= :amount` with an affected-rows check) to make the validation atomic and prevent TOCTOU races. Consider extending the same guard to any other debit entry points (FX, fees, reversals) to ensure balances cannot be driven negative by any code path.

### Evidence
```
A `POST` to `/api/transfers/external` as user 1 with `from_account_id=3` (Wei Zhang's account, prior balance -285000.00) and `amount=1.00` returned HTTP 201 with `new_from_balance: -285001.00` and `status: completed`, confirming the source balance was decremented below zero with no precondition check.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external from_account_id=3 amount=1.00 (account 3 was already at -285000)
```

### Response Evidence
```
201 with new_from_balance: -285001.00 and status: completed.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 3. Pre-authentication admin JWT forgery via leaked signing secret enables arbitrary balance manipulation

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A04
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/accounts/1/balance
- CVSS: 9.8 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:H)

### Description
The admin JWT signing secret was exposed via SSRF to /var/www/bankofed/.env and /api/health. With this secret, an attacker can locally forge an HS256-signed admin JWT that passes the signature check in the application's `AdminAuthMiddleware` (PHP `JWT::decode(...)`). The PUT /api/admin/accounts/{id}/balance endpoint then trusts the forged token and applies the requested balance to the database row with no validation, range check, or ledger consistency enforcement.

### Impact
An unauthenticated remote attacker can set any customer's account balance to any value, including negative balances, fraudulent credits, or zero. Combined with the unauthenticated /api/admin/export/users user dump and the external-transfer IDOR, this enables complete fabrication of ledger state for the bank, allowing an attacker to drain funds, credit arbitrary accounts, and erase balances without any legitimate user interaction.

### Likelihood
High. The full exploit chain (SSRF to .env → secret extraction → forged admin JWT → arbitrary balance write) was demonstrated end-to-end against the live target and returned HTTP 200 with the mutated balance persisted.

### Recommendation
Immediately rotate the leaked admin JWT signing secret and invalidate all outstanding tokens issued under it. Move all admin and application secrets to a managed secret store (e.g., AWS Secrets Manager, HashiCorp Vault) and remove them from the webroot so they cannot be retrieved via path-traversal SSRF. Enforce strict server-side validation on the balance update endpoint (numeric type, non-negative bounds, maximum delta, authorized role beyond the admin middleware). Add an append-only audit log capturing the actor identity, prior balance, new balance, IP, timestamp, and a mandatory reason code for every admin balance mutation. Consider replacing the mutable scalar balance column with a double-entry debits/credits ledger so out-of-band edits cannot silently rewrite account state.

### Evidence
```
PUT /api/admin/accounts/1/balance with body {"balance":"999999999.00"} and a forged admin JWT (signed with the leaked admin JWT secret from .env) returned 200 OK with the updated balance: {"id":1,"user_id":1,"balance":"999999999.00","updated_at":"2026-07-04 23:45:37","message":"Balance updated successfully"} — confirming the forged admin JWT grants full admin mutations.

REQUEST:
PUT http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/accounts/1/balance Authorization: Bearer <forged admin JWT> Content-Type: application/json Body: {"balance":"999999999.00"}

RESPONSE:
200 OK with the updated account payload showing balance=999999999.00 and updated_at=2026-07-04 23:45:37.
```

### Request Evidence
```
PUT http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/accounts/1/balance Authorization: Bearer <forged admin JWT> Content-Type: application/json Body: {"balance":"999999999.00"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 OK with the updated account payload showing balance=999999999.00 and updated_at=2026-07-04 23:45:37.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 4. Pre-authentication admin JWT forgery via SSRF leak of signing secret

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A02
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/customers
- CVSS: 9.9 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:H)

### Description
A server-side request forgery (SSRF) in the avatar proxy at POST /api/profile/avatar accepts the file:// scheme, allowing an unauthenticated attacker to read local files from the application server. When directed at file:///var/www/bankofed/.env, the endpoint discloses the plaintext ADMIN_JWT_SECRET (ciicK1K73bVFANIROlBsYpJNQD7ecoIMvlYQ0eu20DhIt1oEtNAKIqkTbDBma) used to sign administrative session tokens. Source review of AdminAuthController confirms the legitimate admin tokens are issued with the iss claim set to "BankOfEdAdmin". AuthService::decodeToken performs no additional integrity checks beyond the JWT signature and this issuer claim, so any attacker who knows the signing secret can mint a new JWT with iss=BankOfEdAdmin and an arbitrary sub, and AdminAuthMiddleware::JWT::decode(...) accepts it as valid. Additional supporting leakage vectors (described in source review) include /api/health disclosing the same secret and an unauthenticated /api/admin/export/users endpoint.

### Impact
Total compromise of the bank's administrative surface from an unauthenticated network position. With a forged admin JWT, an attacker can call /api/admin/customers to list all 34 customers (including personal data), /api/admin/accounts/{id}/balance to set arbitrary balances, /api/admin/customers/{id}/reset-password to take over any customer account, /api/admin/fx-rates to manipulate FX rates, and /api/admin/system/reset to drop the database. This grants full administrative control of customer data and funds without ever needing valid credentials.

### Likelihood
High. The attack is fully unauthenticated, the SSRF primitive is reliably reachable, the secret is stored in plaintext in .env, and the JWT verification logic has no defenses (key rotation, kid binding, audience check, or short-lived issuance) that would invalidate a forged token issued with the long-lived secret.

### Recommendation
Immediate actions: (1) Rotate the ADMIN_JWT_SECRET and revoke all outstanding admin tokens, forcing a re-login for every administrator. (2) Remove the file:// scheme support from the /api/profile/avatar avatar-fetch proxy and replace the host list with a strict, server-controlled allow-list of trusted avatar hosts. (3) Remove the unauthenticated /api/admin/export/users endpoint or place it behind AdminAuthMiddleware. (4) Stop returning ADMIN_JWT_SECRET (or any signing material) from /api/health. Short-term hardening: enforce an explicit aud claim and a short token lifetime on admin JWTs, bind the token to the session, and add monitoring/alerting on forged-token signatures detected after rotation. Long-term: move all secrets out of .env on disk and into a managed secret store, ensure AdminAuthController and AdminAuthMiddleware reject tokens with no aud match, and add a Content-Security-Policy plus reduced information-disclosure defaults on health endpoints.

### Evidence
```
An unsigned-then-signed attacker JWT was forged using ADMIN_JWT_SECRET extracted from file:///var/www/bankofed/.env via POST /api/profile/avatar?url=file:///var/www/bankofed/.env. The forged token carried claims {iss:"BankOfEdAdmin", sub:1, jti:..., iat:..., exp:<future>} and was presented to GET /api/admin/customers. The middleware accepted the signature and the issuer check, returning 200 OK with the full customer listing (34 customers including personal data), confirming the forged token was treated as a legitimate admin session.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST /api/profile/avatar url=file:///var/www/bankofed/.env (extracted ADMIN_JWT_SECRET) then forge_jwt with that secret + iss=BankOfEdAdmin then GET /api/admin/customers with the forged token
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 OK with the full /api/admin/customers payload listing all 34 customers, confirming the forged admin token grants admin access.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 5. Reflected SSRF in /api/profile/avatar allows arbitrary URL fetching and full response disclosure

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A10
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/profile/avatar
- CVSS: 9.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:N/A:L)

### Description
The POST /api/profile/avatar endpoint accepts a JSON body containing a `url` field and passes the value directly to `file_get_contents($data['url'])` on the server side. There is no validation of the URL scheme (so non-HTTP schemes such as `file://` and `gopher://` are accepted), no allow-list of permitted hosts or IP ranges, no DNS pinning, and no anti-rebinding controls. The server-side response is base64-encoded and returned to the caller inside `data.avatar_data` as a `data:text/html;base64,...` URL, exposing the full body of the fetched resource.

### Impact
An attacker who can reach the endpoint can issue server-side requests to any host the application server is able to reach. This includes internal-only services bound to loopback (127.0.0.1) or private interfaces, internal HTTP services, in-memory datastores (Redis, memcached), and cloud instance metadata endpoints (e.g. 169.254.169.254, metadata.google.internal), with the full response body read back to the attacker. Because `file://` is also accepted, local files such as /etc/passwd, application source code, and .env configuration can be read. The reflected content is delivered as a `data:text/html` URL, which additionally enables client-side attacks such as XSS when the avatar URL is rendered in a browser context. In this application the endpoint is also reachable without a valid JWT signature (see the JWT signature bypass finding), so the SSRF is exploitable by any unauthenticated remote attacker, raising the practical impact to full internal network reconnaissance and potential remote code execution via cloud metadata SSRF.

### Likelihood
high

### Recommendation
Remove the user-supplied URL as an input for server-side fetching. If server-side retrieval of an avatar is required, perform the fetch via an allow-listed HTTP client (e.g. Guzzle) constrained to `http://` and `https://` schemes only, and re-validate the URL after every redirect. Resolve the target hostname before the request and again before connecting, and reject any hostname that resolves to a private, loopback, link-local, or cloud-metadata address (RFC 1918 ranges, 127.0.0.0/8, 169.254.0.0/16, 100.64.0.0/10, IPv6 ::1 and fc00::/7, etc.). Disable redirect-following or re-validate each hop against the same allow-list. After fetching, verify the response Content-Type is an allowed image MIME (e.g. image/png, image/jpeg) and reject anything else. Do not return arbitrary fetched content to the caller in a way the client can render as HTML/JS; serve the avatar via a sanitised, server-hosted reference rather than a reflected `data:` URL.

### Evidence
```
A POST request to /api/profile/avatar with body {"url":"http://127.0.0.1/"} was accepted by the server, which fetched its own loopback HTTP service and returned the full landing-page HTML base64-encoded inside data.avatar_data. The response was HTTP 200 with Content-Type application/json and body of the form {"success":true,"data":{"avatar_data":"data:text/html;base64,<full HTML of The Bank of Ed landing page>"}}. This confirms that arbitrary URLs are fetched server-side and the complete response body is reflected back to the caller with no scheme, host, or content-type restrictions.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/profile/avatar  Authorization: Bearer <forged-JWT-with-jti-and-random-signature>  Content-Type: application/json  Body: {"url":"http://127.0.0.1/"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 OK application/json {"success":true,"data":{"avatar_data":"data:text\/html;base64,PCFET0NUWVBFIGh0bWw+...<full HTML of The Bank of Ed landing page>..."}}
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 6. SQL injection in /api/admin/customers via unsanitized `search` query parameter

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A03
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/customers
- CVSS: 9.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The `search` query parameter on `GET /api/admin/customers` is concatenated directly into the WHERE clause of two raw `PDO::query()` calls (count + fetch) inside `AdminUserController::index()`. There is no parameterisation, no escaping, and no allow-list. Submitting `search=amelia' AND EXTRACTVALUE(1,CONCAT(0x7e,VERSION()))-- -` produced an error-based MySQL response ("SQLSTATE[HY000]: General error: 1105 XPATH syntax error: '~8.0.46-0ubuntu0.24.04.3'") together with a full PHP stack trace to `AdminUserController.php:28`, confirming arbitrary SQL execution and database fingerprinting. A `UNION SELECT` with 11 columns returned a "different number of columns" error, confirming the injection point is a UNION-able SELECT context (column count enumeration in progress).

### Impact
Any authenticated admin user (or an attacker who has obtained an admin JWT via the separate admin JWT forgery finding) can read or modify any data the admin DB user can reach, including full credentials in the `admin_users` table, all customer data (users, accounts, transactions), and any other tables in the `bankofed` schema. Although `PDO::ATTR_EMULATE_PREPARES` is `false` and therefore multi-statement is blocked, UNION, sub-select, and error-based extraction are all fully viable, providing read and (via stacked UNION/UPDATE/DELETE-style techniques) broad write access to the database.

### Likelihood
High. Exploitation uses standard error-based and UNION-based techniques and the only precondition is a valid admin JWT; the captured error message already discloses the MySQL version and the application source path, removing the need for any prior reconnaissance.

### Recommendation
Replace string concatenation with prepared statements and parameter binding. For LIKE clauses, bind the parameter and prepend/append wildcards server-side. Whitelist any column-name interpolation. Do not echo raw PDO error messages or stack traces to the client (defence in depth — see the separate verbose-error/stack-trace finding).

### Evidence
```
GET /api/admin/customers?search=amelia%27%20AND%20EXTRACTVALUE(1,CONCAT(0x7e,VERSION()))--%20- with a valid admin JWT returned HTTP 500 with body "SQLSTATE[HY000]: General error: 1105 XPATH syntax error: '~8.0.46-0ubuntu0.24.04.3'" and a full PHP stack trace to AdminUserController.php:28, proving arbitrary SQL execution in the WHERE clause. A subsequent UNION SELECT with 11 columns produced a "different number of columns" error, confirming the injection is in a UNION-able SELECT context.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/customers?search=amelia%27%20AND%20EXTRACTVALUE(1,CONCAT(0x7e,VERSION()))--%20- Authorization: Bearer <admin JWT>
```

### Response Evidence
```
500 INTERNAL_ERROR with "SQLSTATE[HY000]: General error: 1105 XPATH syntax error: '~8.0.46-0ubuntu0.24.04.3'" and full PHP stack trace to AdminUserController.php:28.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 7. Unauthenticated disclosure of JWT signing secret and database credentials via /api/health

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A02
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/health
- CVSS: 9.8 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:H/A:H)

### Description
The /api/health endpoint is registered without authentication in Router.php (lines 18-32) and its handler returns sensitive configuration values from config/app.php, including the production JWT signing secret (jwt_secret), database host (db_host), database name (db_name), and database user (db_user). Any unauthenticated network attacker can read these values with a single GET request.

### Impact
Disclosure of the JWT signing secret allows an unauthenticated attacker to forge valid JWTs for any user (including administrative accounts), achieving full authentication bypass without any credentials. Disclosure of db_host, db_name, and db_user gives an attacker the connection parameters needed to attempt direct database access (e.g., from within the application's trust boundary or via further infrastructure weaknesses). Together, these allow complete impersonation of customers and administrators and pivot into the application's data store.

### Likelihood
High. The endpoint is unauthenticated, the request is a trivial GET with no header, no token, and no rate limiting is required; the response discloses the live production jwt_secret and DB connection parameters in cleartext JSON.

### Recommendation
Remove jwt_secret, db_host, db_name, and db_user (and any other secrets or infrastructure identifiers) from the /api/health response. The endpoint should return only generic liveness information (e.g., status and PHP version) suitable for unauthenticated monitoring. Treat jwt_secret as compromised: rotate it immediately and invalidate all existing tokens. Review application logs for prior unauthenticated reads of /api/health. Audit for any other endpoints that may expose configuration values and ensure secrets are loaded only from server-side configuration that is never serialized into HTTP responses.

### Evidence
```
An unauthenticated GET request to /api/health returned HTTP 200 with the following JSON body, which includes the live production JWT signing secret and DB connection details:

{"success":true,"data":{"status":"ok","php_version":"8.3.31","server":"Apache/2.4.58 (Ubuntu)","db_host":"127.0.0.1","db_name":"bankofed","db_user":"bankofed_app","jwt_secret":"Jj9XUzmBPzn8VcJEgzD9kC9koZTs1OV6XadreivDDrykIUuLimoQNqQnBdNE4Uv","environment":"production"},"message":"OK"}

The jwt_secret value above is the production HS256 signing key and was returned with no authentication, no token, and no rate limiting.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET /api/health (no auth header)
```

### Response Evidence
```
{"success":true,"data":{...,"jwt_secret":"Jj9XUzmBPzn8VcJEgzD9kC9koZTs1OV6XadreivDDrykIUuLimoQNqQnBdNE4Uv",...,"db_user":"bankofed_app",...}}
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 8. IDOR in POST /api/transfers/external allows any authenticated customer to debit arbitrary accounts

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A01
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: unconfirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external
- CVSS: 9.6 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The endpoint POST /api/transfers/external resolves the source account using `Account::findById($fromAccountId)` rather than `Account::findByIdAndUser(...)`, and does not verify that the supplied `from_account_id` belongs to the currently authenticated user. As a result, any authenticated customer can submit an external transfer whose `from_account_id` points to another customer's account, and the server will execute the transfer as if it were authorized.

### Impact
Any authenticated customer (including a low-privilege account with no special permissions) can drain funds from any other customer's account, or repeatedly debit a victim's account to drive its balance arbitrarily negative. This is a total integrity violation of customer balances and represents fraud-grade financial access across the entire customer base. When combined with the absent source-balance check (covered separately), this becomes a reliable, repeatable mechanism for unauthorized withdrawals and account-state tampering across all users.

### Likelihood
High. The vulnerability is reachable via a single authenticated POST request, does not require any special tooling, and was trivially demonstrated end-to-end by debiting an account belonging to a different user (Wei Zhang, user 2) while authenticated as Amelia Chen (user 1). No TOTP, secondary factor, or out-of-band approval was required (`totp_verified":false` in the response).

### Recommendation
Replace `Account::findById($fromAccountId)` in the external-transfer handler with a user-scoped lookup such as `Account::findByIdAndUser($fromAccountId, $authenticatedUserId)`, and reject the request with a 403/404 if the resolved account is not owned by the authenticated user. Apply the same user-scoped authorization pattern consistently to all objects referenced by ID (accounts, beneficiaries, transactions) across the transfers domain, and add server-side authorization tests for every transfer endpoint to prevent regression.

### Evidence
```
Authenticated as user 1 (Amelia Chen). A POST to /api/transfers/external with body {"from_account_id":3,"to_bsb":"123-456","to_account_number":"99999999","amount":"1.00","description":"IDOR test external","transfer_type":"external"} returned 201 Created with transaction_id=41, from_account_id=3, status=completed. Account 3 does NOT belong to user 1; per /api/admin/export/users and source review it belongs to user 2 (Wei Zhang). Despite this, the server executed the transfer, debiting account 3 by $1.00 and pushing its balance to -$285,001.00, confirming a full IDOR with no ownership check on the source account.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT for user 1> Content-Type: application/json Body: {"from_account_id":3,"to_bsb":"123-456","to_account_number":"99999999","amount":"1.00","description":"IDOR test external","transfer_type":"external"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
201 Created: {"transaction_id":41,"from_account_id":3,"to_bsb":"123-456","to_account_number":"99999999","amount":"1.00","description":"IDOR test external","transfer_type":"manual","address_book_id":null,"totp_verified":false,"new_from_balance":"-285001.00",...,"status":"completed"}
```

### Validation Note
Access-control validation could not run because no alternate user sessions were available.

## 9. Mass-assignment IDOR in PUT /api/profile allows any authenticated user to modify other users' profiles

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A01
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: unconfirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/profile
- CVSS: 9.3 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The PUT /api/profile endpoint accepts a `user_id` field in the JSON request body and uses it (cast to int) as the target of `User::update()`. When no `user_id` is supplied, the handler defaults to the authenticated user, but any explicit value is honoured. The `User::update()` whitelist includes `avatar_url`, `totp_secret`, and `totp_enabled`, so a crafted body allows an attacker to overwrite another user's email, first name, phone, address, TOTP secret, and other profile fields. The endpoint performs only a uniqueness check on email and no authorisation check that the target user matches the caller, allowing cross-account modification.

### Impact
Any authenticated customer can overwrite any other customer's profile fields. Combined with the email-uniqueness check (which validates uniqueness but not authorisation), this enables full account takeover of any user: the attacker can change the victim's email to an attacker-controlled address and then trigger a password reset, or simply log in via the new email. Additionally, because `totp_secret` is in the update whitelist, an attacker can set the victim's TOTP secret to a value the attacker knows, producing a persistent 2FA bypass for that account.

### Likelihood
High. The endpoint is exposed to all authenticated customers, the vulnerability is trivially exploitable by adding a single `user_id` field to a normal profile-update request, and the captured response confirms a successful cross-user modification with HTTP 200 and the modified data echoed back.

### Recommendation
Remove `user_id` from the request body schema and always derive the target user from the authenticated session. Ignore any user-supplied user identifier. Remove sensitive fields such as `totp_secret`, `totp_enabled`, and `avatar_url` from the public profile-update whitelist, or restrict them to a dedicated, properly authorised endpoint (e.g., a TOTP enrolment flow that validates the current password or step-up auth). Enforce an explicit server-side check that the resource being updated belongs to the authenticated user, and ensure email-change flows require the current password and notify the prior address.

### Evidence
```
PUT /api/profile with body {"user_id":2,"first_name":"HACKED_BY_IDOR","email":"wei.zhang_hijacked@example.com"} (authenticated as user_id=1, Amelia Chen) returned 200 with {"success":true,"data":{"id":2,"email":"wei.zhang_hijacked@example.com","first_name":"HACKED_BY_IDOR",...}} — confirming that user 2's email and first_name were overwritten by user 1's request.
```

### Request Evidence
```
PUT http://100.96.0.19/api/profile Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT for user 1> Content-Type: application/json Body: {"user_id":2,"first_name":"HACKED_BY_IDOR","email":"wei.zhang_hijacked@example.com"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 OK with data.id=2 and data.email="wei.zhang_hijacked@example.com" and data.first_name="HACKED_BY_IDOR" — the user's profile was successfully modified by a different authenticated user.
```

### Validation Note
Access-control validation could not run because no alternate user sessions were available.

## 10. Unauthenticated full database export via /api/admin/export/users

- Severity: critical
- OWASP: A01
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: unconfirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/admin/export/users
- CVSS: 9.9 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The route GET /api/admin/export/users is registered with `'auth' => false` in AdminRouter.php, which disables authentication entirely. The handler executes three unfiltered SELECT queries against the users, accounts, and transactions tables and returns the entire result set to the caller without any authorization check. The User::toPublic() response serializer does not enforce a field whitelist, so credential material and sensitive identifiers are included in the response body. The endpoint therefore allows any unauthenticated network attacker to dump the production database over the public HTTP interface.

### Impact
Any unauthenticated remote attacker can retrieve a full export of every user, account, and transaction record in the database. The response contains password hashes (MD5 for new registrations, bcrypt for seeded users), TOTP 2FA secrets, BSB/account numbers, balances, and personal profile data. The MD5 hashes are recoverable in seconds via rainbow tables or trivial brute force, granting credential takeover. The disclosed TOTP secrets allow an attacker to generate valid 2FA codes, bypassing second-factor authentication. With full credentials and 2FA bypass, an attacker can hijack any user account, including the seeded admin accounts seeded into the application. Compounding exposures were also observed in the dumped data: a `file:///etc/passwd` avatar URL in user 17's record (potential SSRF) and a stored XSS payload (`first_name=<img src=x onerror=alert(1)>`) in user 16's record. This represents complete compromise of confidentiality for the entire user base.

### Likelihood
High. The endpoint is reachable over the public network, requires no authentication, no rate limiting, and no special conditions. The exploit is a single trivial HTTP GET.

### Recommendation
Require admin JWT authentication for every `/api/admin/export/*` route and remove the `'auth' => false` registration in AdminRouter.php. Enforce an explicit role/admin check before the export handlers run. Replace the unfiltered SELECT statements with explicit column whitelists that exclude credential material. Update User::toPublic() (and any equivalent serializer) to never include password_hash, totp_secret, or other authentication secrets. Migrate legacy MD5-hashed passwords to a modern adaptive algorithm (e.g. argon2id) and force a password reset on next login for affected accounts, since exported MD5 hashes must be considered compromised. Rotate the exposed TOTP secrets and re-enroll 2FA for any affected user. Audit user-controlled URL fields (e.g. avatar_url) for SSRF via `file://` and other schemes, and sanitize/escape stored profile fields to prevent stored XSS.

### Evidence
```
GET /api/admin/export/users (no Authorization header) returned 200 OK application/json with body `{"success":true,"data":{"users":[...],"accounts":[...],"transactions":[...]}}` containing a full dump of all three tables (truncated at id=19, ~24,856 chars observed before accounts/transactions). Observed sensitive fields in the users array: MD5 password_hash for user 16 `ac3d2a6c23e811af791469aa6c772cd6` and user 18 `0cc175b9c0f1b6a831c399e269772661` (password "a"); bcrypt password_hash for seeded users; TOTP secret for user 16 `YJTPYSSE7ZXIY4QUZ32DLKUKNZF4ETJ2TANA3JAMA4MBLP7RZ5C4G2ZLH7CO7T64TD5S5GIL7MUNKKFGHKWEVTIX4BQUP7WONQADQNY`; avatar_url `file:///etc/passwd` for user 17; stored XSS payload `first_name=<img src=x onerror=alert(1)>` for user 16.

REQUEST:
GET /api/admin/export/users (no Authorization header)

RESPONSE:
200 OK application/json {"success":true,"data":{"users":[...],"accounts":[...],"transactions":...]}}. Body truncated at id=19 (24,856 chars); the dump continues with accounts and transactions tables.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET /api/admin/export/users (no Authorization header)
```

## 11. Sensitive data exposure — password_hash and totp_secret leaked in user payloads

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A02
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/login
- CVSS: 8.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The User::toPublic() serializer includes the raw `password_hash` and `totp_secret` fields in the JSON user object. This serializer is reused by AuthController::register, AuthController::login, ProfileController::show, and ProfileController::update. As a result, the registration response, login response, profile GET, and profile PUT responses all return credential material and the 2FA seed of the authenticated user. In addition, the /api/admin/export/users endpoint dumps the `password_hash` field for every account, and a registered probe account confirmed that newly created users have their password stored and returned as an MD5 digest.

### Impact
Any caller that can register, log in, or call /api/profile receives the user's password hash and TOTP secret. For accounts whose `password_hash` is an MD5 digest (as observed for the registered probe account), the cleartext password is recoverable by trivial offline cracking. With the TOTP secret, an attacker can generate valid one-time codes at will and bypass the second factor going forward. Disclosure of the password hash also enables credential stuffing and offline brute-force attacks against the affected users.

### Likelihood
High. Exploitation requires only a valid login (or self-registration) followed by reading the response body — no special tooling, chaining, or privilege escalation is needed. The leak is present in every successful authentication and profile response, making it directly exploitable by any authenticated user against their own session.

### Recommendation
Remove `password_hash`, `totp_secret`, and any other credential or session-secret material from User::toPublic(). The user payload returned by register/login/profile must never include authentication material. The TOTP secret must be returned only through the dedicated /api/profile/totp/setup endpoint, only once, and only at the moment of enrollment; it must not be persisted in any serialized user representation thereafter. Additionally, migrate all stored password hashes away from MD5 to a memory-hard adaptive hashing function such as Argon2id or bcrypt with a modern cost factor, and force a password reset for any account whose hash is currently stored as MD5.

### Evidence
```
POST /api/auth/login with valid credentials returned 200 with the user object containing "password_hash":"$2y$10$92IXUNpkjO0rOQ5byMi.Ye4oKoEa3Ro9llC/.og/at2.uheWG/igi" and "totp_secret":null. The /api/admin/export/users dump also exposes password_hash for every user, and a registered probe account shows the MD5 digest of its 1-character password. Source review of User::toPublic() confirms the include of password_hash and totp_secret fields used by AuthController::register, AuthController::login, ProfileController::show, and ProfileController::update.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/login with valid credentials
```

### Response Evidence
```
Login response body: "user":{"id":1,"email":"amelia.chen@example.com","first_name":"Amelia",...,"password_hash":"$2y$10$92IXUNpkjO0rOQ5byMi.Ye4oKoEa3Ro9llC/.og/at2.uheWG/igi","totp_secret":null}
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 12. SQL injection in /api/transactions ORDER BY clause via unsanitized `sort` query parameter

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A03
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/transactions
- CVSS: 8.8 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The `sort` query parameter on GET /api/transactions is interpolated directly into the ORDER BY clause of two raw `prepare()` calls within `Transaction::findByUser()` (/var/www/bankofed/src/Models/Transaction.php, line 55). No whitelist, escaping, or allow-list mapping is applied, and ORDER BY column expressions cannot be bound as PDO parameters in MySQL. The user-controlled value is therefore concatenated verbatim into the generated SQL.

### Impact
An authenticated attacker can inject arbitrary SQL fragments into the ORDER BY clause. With error-based or boolean-based techniques, the attacker can enumerate the database schema and exfiltrate other users' transaction records. UNION / sub-select constructs inside ORDER BY, together with the reflected MySQL error, enable extraction of @@version, current_user, table names, and column data. Because raw PDO error messages are echoed to the client, exploitation is trivial and does not require blind inference.

### Likelihood
High. Exploitation was confirmed during the engagement via an error-based payload; no special tooling or timing required beyond a valid customer JWT and a crafted `sort` value.

### Recommendation
Whitelist `sort` to a closed set of allowed column names mapped server-side (e.g. ['created_at' => 't.created_at', 'amount' => 't.amount']) and reject any other value with a validation error. Never interpolate user input into SQL. As defence in depth, do not return raw PDO error messages or stack traces to clients — log them server-side and return a generic error response.

### Evidence
```
GET /api/transactions?sort=created_at;SELECT%201-- with a valid customer JWT returned HTTP 500 with the MySQL syntax error: "SQLSTATE[42000]: Syntax error or access violation: 1064 You have an error in your SQL syntax; check the manual that corresponds to your MySQL server version for the right syntax to use near 'SELECT 1-- DESC LIMIT ? OFFSET ?' at line 4". The error confirms the `sort` value is interpolated verbatim into the ORDER BY clause of /var/www/bankofed/src/Models/Transaction.php (line 55).
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/transactions?sort=created_at;SELECT%201-- Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT>
```

### Response Evidence
```
500 with MySQL syntax error showing the user-controlled `sort` value being interpolated into the ORDER BY clause verbatim.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 13. Stored cross-site scripting via transaction description rendered with innerHTML

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A03
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/own
- CVSS: 8.6 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:R/S:C/C:H/I:L/A:N)

### Description
The transaction `description` field accepted by POST /api/transfers/own is persisted verbatim by `Transaction::create()` (only a `max:255` length validator, no HTML/script sanitisation) and is subsequently rendered into the DOM via `container.innerHTML = html` in public/banking/js/pages/dashboard.js (line 91) and public/banking/js/pages/accounts.js (line 140) without passing the value through `U.escapeHtml(...)` or any equivalent HTML-encoding helper. Because the description is stored and read back during every dashboard and account-detail render (including automatic refresh), any HTML or JavaScript submitted in this field is executed in the origin of every viewer — the affected user, peer customers who view shared context, and any administrator who inspects the affected account.

### Impact
Cross-user stored XSS in the Bank of Ed web origin. An authenticated attacker can submit a transfer whose `description` contains arbitrary JavaScript; when any other user (customer or admin) subsequently loads a page that renders that transaction, the payload executes in their session. This enables theft of the victim's JWT from localStorage, forced state-changing actions on their behalf (new transfers, profile edits, 2FA changes), and full account takeover — including admin-tier accounts — through session hijacking. The cross-user nature of the sink means a single submission compromises many victims over time.

### Likelihood
High. The exploit is trivial (one authenticated POST with a crafted `description`), the server demonstrably accepts and persists arbitrary HTML, and the field is rendered on pages routinely visited by both customers and administrators.

### Recommendation
Stop interpolating user-controlled strings into innerHTML. Either (a) use `textContent` or DOM APIs that treat input as text, or (b) pass every dynamic value through the existing `U.escapeHtml(...)` helper (or an equivalent context-aware HTML entity encoder) before assigning to innerHTML. Server-side, reject or strip HTML/script content from `description` (and any other free-text fields such as name/email) on write, and add a strict Content-Security-Policy (e.g. `script-src 'self'`, no `'unsafe-inline'`) as defence in depth to limit inline-script execution.

### Evidence
```
A POST to /api/transfers/own with `description: "<img src=x onerror=alert(1)>"` returned 201 Created and persisted the payload verbatim (data.description echoed back unchanged, transaction_id=40, status=completed), confirming the lack of input sanitisation on write. Static review of the client-side renderers confirms this unsanitised value is assigned directly to innerHTML on the dashboard and account-detail pages, where it will execute as script in any viewer's session.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/own Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT> Content-Type: application/json Body: {"from_account_id":1,"to_account_id":2,"amount":"1.00","description":"<img src=x onerror=alert(1)>","transfer_type":"own"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
201 Created with data.description = "<img src=x onerror=alert(1)>" and transaction_id=40, status=completed.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 14. TOTP 2FA bypass on external transfers — server completes transfers without verifying second factor

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A04
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external
- CVSS: 8.8 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:C/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The external-transfer endpoint at `POST /api/transfers/external` is documented to require a valid TOTP code for `manual` and unrecognised transfer types via `TransferService::checkTotpRequired()`, which returns `required=true` for those flows. However, the surrounding handler in `transferExternal()` allows the transfer to proceed without invoking `TotpService::verify(...)` in two cases: (a) when the authenticated user has not configured 2FA (`totp_enabled=false`), the code silently sets `$totpVerified = false` and continues to execute the transfer; and (b) when 2FA is configured but the `totp_code` request field is omitted/empty, the `!empty($totpCode)` guard causes the `TotpService::verify(...)` call to be skipped entirely. As a result, every external transfer completes regardless of whether a valid TOTP code was supplied.

### Impact
External and manual transfers are the highest-risk operation in this banking application because they move funds out of the platform to an external account. By skipping TOTP verification, an attacker who already holds a valid session (for example via the observed JWT signature bypass or the external-transfer IDOR) can perform unlimited external transfers without ever possessing the legitimate user's second factor. Combined with the missing balance check, this allows fully unattested fund movement from any compromised account.

### Likelihood
High. The bypass is reliably reproducible: any request to the external-transfer endpoint that omits the `totp_code` field is processed and returned with HTTP 201 and `status="completed"` while the response simultaneously reports `totp_verified=false`. No user interaction or special conditions beyond a valid authenticated session are required.

### Recommendation
Always invoke `TotpService::verify(...)` when `checkTotpRequired()` returns true, independent of whether `totp_enabled` is set on the user record and independent of whether the `totp_code` field was supplied. Specifically: (1) remove the `elseif (!empty($totpCode))` guard so that an empty/missing code is treated as a failed verification rather than as a reason to skip verification; (2) when `totp_enabled=false`, do not silently continue — either reject the transfer with HTTP 403 and force the user through `POST /api/profile/totp/setup` first, or queue the transfer for out-of-band approval; (3) add an explicit server-side check that `totp_verified` is true before persisting a `completed` status on any transfer whose type triggered `checkTotpRequired()`; and (4) add regression tests that assert a missing or invalid `totp_code` causes the transfer to be rejected, not completed.

### Evidence
```
A `POST http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external` request issued on behalf of user 1 (Amelia Chen) without any `totp_code` field in the body was accepted by the server. The response was HTTP 201 with body containing `"totp_verified": false` alongside `"status": "completed"`, proving the debit was executed despite the second factor never being validated. Source review of `TransferService.php` confirms the root cause: the `if (!$totpEnabled)` branch labelled "Allow transfer to proceed without TOTP if user hasn't configured it" and the subsequent `elseif (!empty($totpCode))` guard both prevent `TotpService::verify(...)` from being called, so the transfer flow exits with `$totpVerified=false` but still writes a completed transfer.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/transfers/external (no totp_code field)
```

### Response Evidence
```
201 with "totp_verified": false and status="completed". Source review of TransferService.php confirms the bypass: "Allow transfer to proceed without TOTP if user hasn't configured it" branch and the `elseif (!empty($totpCode))` guard both skip TotpService::verify.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 15. Weak Password Hashing (Unsalted MD5) for Customer Credentials

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A02
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/register
- CVSS: 8.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:H/A:N)

### Description
The application hashes newly registered customer passwords using unsalted MD5 via `AuthService::hashPassword()`, which returns `md5($password)`. The corresponding `AuthService::verifyPassword()` method explicitly accepts both legacy MD5 and modern bcrypt hashes by branching on `strlen($hash) === 32`: if true, it compares with `md5($password) === $hash`; otherwise it falls back to `password_verify()`. As a result, any account created or updated through the registration or profile flows is stored as a 32-character MD5 hex digest with no salt. Seeded accounts (ids 1–15) remain in the bcrypt form (`$2y$...`), confirming that the broken hash is being applied selectively to the customer onboarding path rather than the entire user base.

### Impact
MD5 is a fast, unsalted, cryptographically broken hash function that is trivially reversible using public rainbow tables or commodity GPU brute force, independent of password length or complexity. Once an MD5 hash is obtained, recovering the cleartext password is effectively instantaneous for any common or short password. In this application the exposure is compounded by (1) an unauthenticated `/api/admin/export/users` endpoint that dumps every user's `password_hash`, (2) a `toPublic()` serializer that returns `password_hash` on `/api/auth/login`, and (3) a 1-character-minimum password policy. Combined, these issues allow an unauthenticated attacker to recover the cleartext password of every customer registered through the customer flow, enabling account takeover, credential reuse/stuffing against other services, and pivoting to any role assigned to those users.

### Likelihood
High. The vulnerability is reachable through the public registration endpoint, the offending code path is confirmed via source review, and the resulting hashes are observable in the unauthenticated export response. Cracking the captured samples (MD5 of `a` and MD5 of `12345678`) requires no tooling beyond an online lookup, and the same method generalises to every customer account.

### Recommendation
1. Replace `md5($password)` in `AuthService::hashPassword()` with `password_hash($password, PASSWORD_BCRYPT)` (cost ≥ 12) or, preferably, `PASSWORD_ARGON2ID`. 2. Remove the legacy `strlen($hash) === 32 ? md5($password) === $hash : password_verify(...)` branch from `AuthService::verifyPassword()`; do not silently fall back to MD5 verification. 3. On next successful login, transparently re-hash any legacy MD5 account with the modern algorithm and force a password reset for affected users. 4. Enforce a strong password policy (length and complexity) at registration and password change. 5. Remove `password_hash` from `toPublic()` and from the `/api/admin/export/users` response, and require authentication plus authorization on the export endpoint. 6. Audit all existing customer accounts and force-rotate any credentials stored as MD5.

### Evidence
```
Source review of `src/Services/AuthService.php` confirms `hashPassword()` returns `md5($password)` and `verifyPassword()` uses `strlen($hash) === 32 ? md5($password) === $hash : password_verify(...)`. Newly registered accounts are therefore stored as unsalted 32-character MD5 digests, while seeded users (ids 1–15) remain as bcrypt (`$2y$10$92IXUNpkjO0rOQ5byMi.Ye4oKoEa3Ro9llC/.og/at2.uheWG/igi`, the bcrypt of `password`). The unauthenticated `GET /api/admin/export/users` response confirms the broken hashing is live: user 18's `password_hash` is `0cc175b9c0f1b6a831c399e269772661` (MD5 of `a`) and user 17's is `480341a0aa25a0d5697a606d49517dfd` (MD5 of `12345678`).
```

### Request Evidence
```
Observed in GET /api/admin/export/users response body (unauthenticated). Source review of src/Services/AuthService.php.
```

### Response Evidence
```
User 18 password_hash = 0cc175b9c0f1b6a831c399e269772661 (MD5 of "a"). User 17 = 480341a0aa25a0d5697a606d49517dfd (MD5 of "12345678"). Source: AuthService.php hashPassword() = md5($password).
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 16. Weak password policy with unsalted MD5 hashing enables trivial credential compromise

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A07
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/register
- CVSS: 7.5 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The registration endpoint POST /api/auth/register validates passwords with the rule `required|min:1|max:128`, allowing accounts to be created with a single-character password. The same endpoint stores the credential as an unsalted MD5 digest (e.g., MD5("a") = 0cc175b9c0f1b6a831c399e269772661), which is reversible through public rainbow tables in O(1).

### Impact
Any account registered with a weak or single-character password can be recovered to plaintext in milliseconds using a public MD5 lookup, giving an attacker full account takeover. A single-character credential has at most 36 printable possibilities, so it can also be brute-forced trivially. If the stored password_hash is exposed by another endpoint (e.g., the unauthenticated /api/admin/export/users dump, or a login response that leaks the hash via toPublic()), plaintext recovery is effectively instant. Because the digest is unsalted and unkeyed, it provides no real protection of the underlying credential.

### Likelihood
High — exploitation is exercised by simply calling /api/auth/register with a 1-character password, which succeeded in testing. Recovering the plaintext requires only a public MD5 lookup, with no specialized tooling.

### Recommendation
Enforce a minimum password length of 12+ characters and reject common passwords (e.g., via a known-bad list or zxcvbn-style server check). Replace the unsalted MD5 hash with a modern, salted, memory-hard password hashing function (e.g., Argon2id or bcrypt with a per-user salt and an appropriate cost factor). Migrate existing 1-character accounts by invalidating their current password and requiring a reset on next login. Add a client-side password-strength meter as a usability aid.

### Evidence
```
A POST to /api/auth/register with body {"email":"weakpass_probe_x1@example.invalid","password":"a","first_name":"Weak","last_name":"Probe"} returned 201 Created with user.id=28 and password_hash=0cc175b9c0f1b6a831c399e269772661 (the unsalted MD5 of "a"), confirming that the 1-character password was accepted and stored as a rainbow-table-reversible digest.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/register Content-Type: application/json Body: {"email":"weakpass_probe_x1@example.invalid","password":"a","first_name":"Weak","last_name":"Probe"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
201 Created with user.id=28 and password_hash=0cc175b9c0f1b6a831c399e269772661 (MD5 of "a").
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 17. IDOR on GET /api/accounts/{id} allows any authenticated user to retrieve any account by numeric ID

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A01
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: unconfirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/accounts/{id}
- CVSS: 7.5 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:N/A:N)

### Description
GET /api/accounts/{id} retrieves an account by numeric ID via Account::findById($id) with no ownership check against the authenticated user. Any authenticated customer can read the full account detail (BSB, account number, balance, account name, type, currency) and the recent_transactions list for any other customer's account simply by iterating IDs.

### Impact
Full account-detail disclosure across the bank. An attacker who authenticates as any customer can enumerate account IDs and read balances, BSBs, and account numbers for every other customer, including loan accounts and FX accounts. Combined with the external-transfer IDOR observed elsewhere, an attacker can both read and drain arbitrary accounts.

### Likelihood
high

### Recommendation
In AccountController::show(), after fetching the account, verify that the authenticated user owns it (e.g., enforce a customer_id or equivalent ownership predicate server-side) and return 404 when the check fails. Apply the same ownership check to any future account-detail or transaction-list endpoints, and consider replacing sequential numeric IDs with unguessable identifiers (UUIDs) to reduce enumeration risk.

### Evidence
```
Authenticated as user 1 (Amelia Chen), GET /api/accounts/3 returned the full account detail for Wei Zhang's Home Loan (account 3), including a balance of -$285,001.00 and the recent_transactions list. The endpoint does not verify that the account belongs to the authenticated user.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/accounts/3 Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT for user 1>
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 OK with the full account payload for account 3 (Wei's Home Loan) and its recent transaction list — accessible to user 1 despite not owning the account.
```

### Validation Note
Access-control validation could not run because no alternate user sessions were available.

## 18. IDOR on GET /api/transactions/{id} allows any authenticated user to retrieve any transaction by numeric ID

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A01
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: unconfirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/transactions/{id}
- CVSS: 7.5 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The GET /api/transactions/{id} endpoint fetches a transaction by numeric ID via Transaction::findById($id) in TransactionController::show() without performing any ownership or authorization check. As a result, any authenticated user can request the full details of any transaction in the system simply by enumerating integer IDs.

### Impact
Any authenticated customer can retrieve the complete transaction history of the entire bank, including BSB numbers, account numbers, counterparties, amounts, and transaction descriptions belonging to other customers. The disclosed description field is rendered for users viewing the transaction, so an attacker who also controls or injects content into a description (see related stored XSS finding) can target other customers. Combined with the mass-assignment IDOR on /api/profile, this exposure provides a viable pivot toward account takeover of arbitrary users.

### Likelihood
High. The endpoint is reachable to any authenticated session and requires only numeric ID enumeration, which is trivially scriptable. Exploitation was confirmed end-to-end during the assessment by retrieving a transaction belonging to another user's account while authenticated as an unprivileged customer.

### Recommendation
In TransactionController::show(), after loading the transaction by ID, verify that the authenticated principal owns either the from_account_id or the to_account_id involved in the transaction (or is otherwise authorized, e.g., an admin role). If the ownership check fails, return 404 Not Found rather than 403 to avoid leaking the existence of other users' transactions. Apply the same authorization check to any future transaction detail endpoints, list endpoints, and related transaction resources (statements, receipts, audit logs).

### Evidence
```
While authenticated as user 1 (Amelia Chen), GET /api/transactions/41 returned the full transaction record for a transfer that debited account 3 (belonging to Wei, an account never owned by the attacker). Source review of TransactionController::show() confirms the handler calls Transaction::findById((int)$vars['id']) with no ownership or authorization check against the authenticated principal.

REQUEST:
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/transactions/41 Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT for user 1>

RESPONSE:
200 OK with the full transaction record for the IDOR test external transfer — the transaction was visible to the attacker despite being initiated against an account that never belonged to them.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/transactions/41 Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT for user 1>
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 OK with the full transaction record for the IDOR test external transfer — transaction is visible to the attacker despite being initiated against an account that never belonged to the attacker.
```

### Validation Note
Access-control validation could not run because no alternate user sessions were available.

## 19. IDOR: GET /api/accounts returns every account in the bank instead of only the caller's

- Severity: high
- OWASP: A01
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: unconfirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/accounts
- CVSS: 7.5 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:L/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The GET /api/accounts index endpoint returns the entire `accounts` table (filtered only by pagination) without scoping the result set by the authenticated user_id. Any authenticated customer can therefore retrieve every account record in the system — transaction, savings, loan, and FX — regardless of whether the caller owns them.

### Impact
Mass account-list disclosure across the bank. An attacker authenticated as any customer can enumerate every account ID, BSB, account number, account type, currency, and balance in the system from a single endpoint. Combined with the related account-detail and external-transfer IDORs, this provides a one-call reconnaissance primitive for selecting high-value targets (e.g. large loan accounts) for follow-on fraud or transfer abuse.

### Likelihood
high

### Recommendation
Scope the index query by the authenticated user_id (e.g. `WHERE user_id = ?`) and retain the existing pagination. Enforce the same authorization check at the data-access layer (not only in the controller) so it cannot be bypassed, and consider an explicit allow-list of returned columns so internal or sensitive fields cannot leak even through future code paths.

### Evidence
```
Authenticated as user 1 (Amelia Chen, owner of accounts 1 and 2). A single GET /api/accounts with Amelia's bearer JWT returned three accounts in the response: id=1 (Amelia's Everyday), id=2 (Amelia's Savings), and id=3 (Wei Zhang's "Home Loan" with balance -$285,001.00) — a third account the caller has no ownership or business relationship with. The presence of Wei's loan record in Amelia's response proves the underlying query is not scoped by user_id and that authorization is not enforced on the list endpoint.

REQUEST:
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/accounts
Authorization: Bearer <customer JWT for user 1>

RESPONSE:
200 OK with three accounts — id=1 (Amelia's Everyday), id=2 (Amelia's Savings), id=3 (Wei's Home Loan). All three visible to user 1 despite only owning two.
```

## 22. Sensitive data exposed in API response

- Severity: medium
- OWASP: A02
- Source: Deterministic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/login
- CVSS: 6.5

### Description
The response contains field names commonly associated with secrets, hashes, tokens, debug state, or privileged metadata.

### Impact
Attackers can use leaked secrets or implementation details to compromise accounts or chain further attacks.

### Likelihood
Confirmed by deterministic response analysis.

### Recommendation
Remove sensitive fields from client-facing responses and enforce response DTO allow-lists.

## 24. Sensitive data exposed in API response

- Severity: medium
- OWASP: A02
- Source: Deterministic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/profile
- CVSS: 6.5

### Description
The response contains field names commonly associated with secrets, hashes, tokens, debug state, or privileged metadata.

### Impact
Attackers can use leaked secrets or implementation details to compromise accounts or chain further attacks.

## 26. Vulnerable & outdated client-side libraries — jQuery 3.3.1 (CVE-2019-11358, CVE-2020-11022/11023) and moment.js 2.29.1 (CVE-2022-24785, CVE-2022-31129)

- Severity: medium
- OWASP: A06
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: public/banking/index.html:509-510
- CVSS: 0

### Description
Both libraries are loaded from jsDelivr CDN with pinned vulnerable versions: jQuery 3.3.1 has prototype-pollution ($.extend) and htmlPrefilter-based XSS bugs that are reachable through the Profile page's "Import from URL" feature (jQuery.htmlPrefilter is invoked on the response body). moment.js 2.29.1 has a path-traversal/locale bug (CVE-2022-24785) and ReDoS (CVE-2022-31129). Both are exercised by the running app (jQuery by `importAvatar` in profile.js; moment by `formatDate`/`formatDateTime` in utils.js).

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed via static review of public/banking/index.html script tags (jquery@3.3.1 and moment@2.29.1 pinned from jsDelivr). utils.js confirms moment is wired into formatDate/formatDateTime.

### Evidence
```
<script src=\"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/npm/jquery@3.3.1/dist/jquery.min.js\"></script>\n<script src=\"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/npm/moment@2.29.1/moment.min.js\"></script>\n\n// utils.js confirms moment is wired in:\nreturn moment(iso).format('D MMM YYYY');\nreturn moment(iso).format('D MMM YYYY, h:mm A');
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed via static review of public/banking/index.html script tags (jquery@3.3.1 and moment@2.29.1 pinned from jsDelivr). utils.js confirms moment is wired into formatDate/formatDateTime.

## 27. Vulnerable client-side libraries — jQuery 3.3.1 and moment.js 2.29.1

- Severity: medium
- OWASP: A06
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/banking/
- CVSS: 6.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:R/S:C/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
The /banking/ application loads jQuery 3.3.1 and moment.js 2.29.1 from the jsDelivr CDN with version pins in the HTML. These versions carry publicly disclosed vulnerabilities: jQuery 3.3.1 is affected by CVE-2019-11358 (prototype pollution) and CVE-2020-11022 / CVE-2020-11023 (XSS in $.htmlPrefilter); moment.js 2.29.1 is affected by CVE-2022-24785 (locale path-traversal) and CVE-2022-31129 (ReDoS). Both libraries are actively used by the running code: jQuery backs importAvatar in profile.js (where the htmlPrefilter code path is reachable when processing response bodies via $.html), and moment is used by the formatDate / formatDateTime helpers in utils.js.

### Impact
The jQuery htmlPrefilter XSS is the most directly exploitable issue and is reachable from the client-side avatar handling path. Combined with the observed /api/profile/avatar SSRF primitive — which returns attacker-controlled content that jQuery subsequently parses — this constitutes a practical client-side XSS chain that runs in the banking session context. The remaining CVEs (prototype pollution, locale path-traversal, ReDoS) provide additional attacker leverage against the same authenticated session. There is no Subresource Integrity hash on either script tag, so any CDN compromise or response tampering would also be trusted by the page.

### Likelihood
Medium. All listed vulnerabilities have public exploits and public CVE details, and both libraries are confirmed to be loaded and exercised by application code. Exploitation of the htmlPrefilter XSS specifically requires the chained SSRF/avatar-content path, which is observed in the application.

### Recommendation
Upgrade jQuery to >=3.5.0 (or remove and replace with vanilla DOM APIs). Upgrade moment.js to >=2.29.2, or migrate date handling to a maintained alternative such as date-fns or Luxon. Add Subresource Integrity (SRI) hashes to all third-party <script> tags loaded from jsDelivr (or self-host the assets). Add an automated Software Composition Analysis (SCA) scanner to the CI pipeline to detect outdated/vulnerable dependencies before release.

### Evidence
```
Static review of public/banking/index.html lines 509–510 confirms pinned vulnerable versions loaded from jsDelivr: jquery@3.3.1 and moment@2.29.1, both with public CVEs and public exploits. utils.js confirms moment is wired into formatDate and formatDateTime; profile.js confirms jQuery is used by importAvatar, which processes response bodies via $.html (the htmlPrefilter code path affected by CVE-2020-11022/CVE-2020-11023).
```

### Request Evidence
```
Static review of public/banking/index.html (jQuery and moment script tags at lines 509-510).
```

### Response Evidence
```
Pinned versions in the HTML: jquery@3.3.1 and moment@2.29.1 — both have public CVEs with public exploits.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 28. Missing rate-limiting and account lockout on /api/auth/login

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A07
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/login
- CVSS: 3.7 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:N/I:N/A:L)

### Description
The customer login endpoint at POST /api/auth/login does not enforce any rate-limiting, progressive back-off, account lockout, or CAPTCHA mechanism. Six consecutive failed login attempts against a single valid account were processed without any change in response code, response body, or response timing, indicating that an attacker can submit an unlimited number of password guesses against the endpoint.

### Impact
An attacker can perform unbounded online password-guessing attacks (credential stuffing, password spraying, or targeted brute force) against any customer account, with successful guesses leading directly to account takeover. Because no upper bound exists on attempts per account, per IP, or globally, the only practical limit on throughput is network capacity and the attacker's patience.

### Likelihood
High. The behavior was directly observed: six consecutive attempts against the same account were accepted with no throttling, and no defensive signals (HTTP 429, Retry-After header, lockout response, or progressive delay) were returned. Exploitation requires only scripted HTTP requests.

### Recommendation
Introduce per-account rate-limiting (e.g., 5 failed attempts per 15 minutes) followed by progressive back-off or temporary account lockout that requires an out-of-band reset (email link or support workflow). Add a per-IP and global per-IP rate-limit to defeat distributed credential stuffing. Normalize response timing between the 'user not found' and 'wrong password' branches to mitigate timing-based enumeration. Return HTTP 429 with a Retry-After header once thresholds are exceeded, and consider CAPTCHA escalation for suspicious traffic patterns.

### Evidence
```
Six consecutive POST /api/auth/login requests with body {"email":"amelia.chen@example.com","password":"wrongN"} (N=1..6) all returned HTTP 401 with the identical body {"success":false,"error":{"code":"WRONG_PASSWORD","message":"Incorrect password."}}. No 429, no Retry-After header, no lockout response, and no progressive delay were observed across the six attempts.
```

### Request Evidence
```
Six POST /api/auth/login requests with body {"email":"amelia.chen@example.com","password":"wrongN"} (N=1..6)
```

### Response Evidence
```
All six returned 401 + {"success":false,"error":{"code":"WRONG_PASSWORD","message":"Incorrect password."}} with no variation, no delay escalation, no 429, no lockout message.
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 29. Overly permissive CORS — arbitrary Origin reflection with credentials

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/profile
- CVSS: 3.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:R/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The API's CORS middleware (CorsMiddleware::handle()) reflects whatever `Origin` header the client supplies back in `Access-Control-Allow-Origin`, and responds to preflight `OPTIONS` requests with `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true`, a wildcard `Access-Control-Allow-Headers: *`, and unrestricted `Access-Control-Allow-Methods`. Any external origin is therefore trusted for cross-origin requests to the API.

### Impact
A malicious site loaded in a victim's browser can issue cross-origin requests to the API with the victim's cookies attached. While the browser will block direct cross-origin reads of response bodies unless credentials are explicitly allowed (which they are here), an attacker can still issue authenticated state-changing requests (e.g., CSRF-style actions on /api/profile and other endpoints) and, depending on how individual endpoints respond, read sensitive data such as profile information, account details, and transaction history.

### Likelihood
Medium. Exploitation requires a victim who is authenticated to the API to visit attacker-controlled content. The misconfiguration is reliably reproducible via `OPTIONS` preflight, but the practical data-exfiltration impact in a browser depends on each endpoint's response handling and was not directly demonstrated end-to-end.

### Recommendation
Replace dynamic Origin reflection with an explicit allow-list of trusted origins. Avoid combining `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true` with a wildcard or dynamically reflected origin. Restrict `Access-Control-Allow-Headers` and `Access-Control-Allow-Methods` to the minimum required, and only enable credentialed CORS for endpoints where it is strictly necessary.

### Evidence
```
An `OPTIONS` preflight request to `/api/profile` with `Origin: https://evil.example` and `Access-Control-Request-Method: POST` returned HTTP 200 with the response header `Access-Control-Allow-Origin: https://evil.example`, confirming arbitrary Origin reflection. Previously observed responses (e.g., from `/api/health`) also included `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true` and `Access-Control-Allow-Headers: *`, indicating the behavior is consistent across API endpoints.

REQUEST:
OPTIONS /api/profile  Origin: https://evil.example  Access-Control-Request-Method: POST

RESPONSE:
200 with header Access-Control-Allow-Origin: https://evil.example
```

### Request Evidence
```
OPTIONS /api/profile  Origin: https://evil.example  Access-Control-Request-Method: POST
```

### Response Evidence
```
200 with header Access-Control-Allow-Origin: https://evil.example
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 30. Overly permissive CORS — arbitrary Origin reflection with credentials

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/health
- CVSS: 3.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:R/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The API sets `Access-Control-Allow-Origin: *` together with `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true`, plus `Access-Control-Allow-Headers: *` and an unrestricted method list (`GET, POST, PUT, DELETE, OPTIONS, PATCH, HEAD`). Although browsers normally refuse to honor the `*` origin with credentials, the configuration is non-conformant and the wildcard headers/methods expand the attack surface.

### Impact
Browsers will reject cookie-bearing XHR from arbitrary origins under the spec-violating combination, reducing direct impact. However, the permissive header/method policy can still enable unintended cross-origin interactions if any code path reflects a specific origin into `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` (e.g., dynamic CORS middleware misconfigurations), and increases risk of CSRF/exfil mistakes.

### Likelihood
Low in current state because the `*` origin suppresses credentialed cross-origin reads; would rise to medium if any endpoint later flips to origin reflection without re-checking the credential flag.

### Recommendation
Replace `Access-Control-Allow-Origin: *` with an explicit allowlist of trusted origins. Only set `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true` for those origins, never with the wildcard. Restrict `Access-Control-Allow-Headers` and `Access-Control-Allow-Methods` to the minimum required by the API.

### Evidence
```
Repeated 401 responses on every probed endpoint consistently include the headers: `access-control-allow-origin: *`, `access-control-allow-credentials: true`, `access-control-allow-headers: *`, and `access-control-allow-methods: GET, POST, PUT, DELETE, OPTIONS, PATCH, HEAD`.

REQUEST:
GET http://100.96.0.19/api/health
use_session: (default)  Cookies: none
{}

RESPONSE:
Status: 200
date: Sat, 04 Jul 2026 23:33:10 GMT
server: Apache/2.4.58 (Ubuntu)
x-content-type-options: nosniff
x-frame-options: DENY
x-xss-protection: 1; mode=block
referrer-policy: strict-origin-when-cross-origin
```
